<div id="review_3202274066" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3202274066" />
    <a title="Lee Foust" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust"><img alt="Lee Foust" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875445p2/13984676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3202274066?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3202274066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2766694808628297500">After listening to a terrific audio book version of <i>Animal Farm</i> on youtube, I found an even more delightful audio book version of this other Orwellian novel and enjoyed the heck out of it--an Australian actor reads it, I believe, judging from his pronunciation of the word "kids." Listening rather than reading may have caused me to pass over some of the more darkly ironic bits (judging from the other reviews here)--also the reader is just so convincing! (It's difficult to laugh at someone when yo</span>
  <span id="freeText2766694808628297500" style="display:none">After listening to a terrific audio book version of <i>Animal Farm</i> on youtube, I found an even more delightful audio book version of this other Orwellian novel and enjoyed the heck out of it--an Australian actor reads it, I believe, judging from his pronunciation of the word "kids." Listening rather than reading may have caused me to pass over some of the more darkly ironic bits (judging from the other reviews here)--also the reader is just so convincing! (It's difficult to laugh at someone when you feel like they're in the room with you telling their story.) Still, I was unexpectedly drawn into this novel, having no expectations, and quickly became hooked on this bored, nostalgic, everyman character.<br /><br />What struck me most was the charming wistfulness of our narrator, George Bowling, and his nostalgia for the miserable, poverty-ridden and unfair world in which he grew up, which is, however, completely understandable as he's now living in a newer, more boring (if perhaps more materially comfortable), tract-home, middle-class post WWI world. (The situation reminded me of the opening of Milan Kundera's <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i>, in which the narrator comes across a book of Holocaust photos and experiences the shame of being prompted by the photos to feel nostalgic for his lost childhood, since it was of the same time period as WWII.) George's situation is as charming as it is tragic, as he pines for his youth although it's filled, to hear him tell it, with misery and uncertainty: the demise of his father's business, his parents' deaths, and the waywardness of his brother.<br /><br />Then comes the absurdity of WWI, which wipes George's old word and personal past aside and sets both he and England on a whole new path...<br /><br />The novel takes an even darker turn, then, when George attends an anti-fascist lecture and the specter of the coming second world war comes to threaten once again to destroy even the boring complacency of the narrator's nostalgia. While he tries to recapture his past with a clandestine trip back to the hometown we've heard so much about, the specter of both modernity and the coming war overshadow everything. Yet George is so irascible and pessimistic, he's hardly fazed. I feel like the novel is both a hymn to and a scathing attack on phlegmatic British stiff-upper-lipness. (A student told me the other day that the Japanese build such fragile paper houses because there are so many natural disasters in Japan, they've adapted to the impermanence in the construction of their dwellings. Seems like the English have adapted to misery and endless war through a combination of pessimism and stoicism, no? "Keep Calm and Carry On--you knew it would be a shit life from the start, Grahame.)<br /><br />This is heavy stuff so winningly, lightly, and charmingly narrated it actually strikes home harder, in the end, than a lot of other more obviously between-the-wars potboilers and texts warning of the evils of fascism. I enjoyed the heck out of it, smiling and wincing by turns.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2766694808628297500" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_188233587" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/188233587" />
    <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi"><img alt="Lorenzo Berardi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417557095p2/183865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/188233587?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorenzo Berardi" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi">Lorenzo Berardi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=a-my-english-library">a-my-english-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer188233587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12674911660742988813">This is my fifth Orwell and the one I liked the most, so far. <br/>I reckon how I should reread (and in English at this time) both "1984" and "Animal Farm" before putting "Coming Up for Air" on top, but at the moment it stands there. <br/><br/>So why have I liked this novel so much? <br/>Oh, there are several and kind of personal reasons. <br/><br/>To begin with, I had the chance to spend some time in the tiny village of Sutton Courtenay where Eric Arthur Blair better known as George Orwell rests. Sutton Courtenay is just a </span>
  <span id="freeText12674911660742988813" style="display:none">This is my fifth Orwell and the one I liked the most, so far. <br />I reckon how I should reread (and in English at this time) both "1984" and "Animal Farm" before putting "Coming Up for Air" on top, but at the moment it stands there. <br /><br />So why have I liked this novel so much? <br />Oh, there are several and kind of personal reasons. <br /><br />To begin with, I had the chance to spend some time in the tiny village of Sutton Courtenay where Eric Arthur Blair better known as George Orwell rests. Sutton Courtenay is just a little corner of Oxfordshire without a single shop, two or three pubs and a church with the graveyard hosting the tombs of Orwell and the former British prime minister Lord Asquith. There are plenty of bunnies popping up in the fields and the funny statue of a dog guarding up the porch of an old mansion. The river Thames flows nearby. A perfectly functioning lock let the boats go by.<br /><br />It's just a coincidence that Orwell ended up in Sutton Courtenay at the end of his way too short days, but as a matter of fact he spent some time wandering and wondering around there when he was a kid.<br /><br />Now, what "Coming Up for Air" is if not an elegy of a similar corner of Oxfordshire? You can really picture young Eric Arthur spending his time fishing along the grassy banks of the river back in the old days and riding his fixed-wheel bicycle up and down a little hill. <br /><br />The imaginary town of Lower Binfield reminded me of Abingdon, where I currently live, with its market square, its High Street, its beer factory chimney and the bygone shops swallowed by the big distribution. <br />The only difference is that Orwell wrote about this "lost England" at the end of the 1930s sighing for how much things changed in a span of only thirty years. <br />And the way this dull, but peaceful Lower Binfield has given way to an industrial, red-bricked town populated by people coming from Lancashire or Staffordshire and with no roots in Oxfordshire is masterfully rendered. <br /><br />I could actually do what George Bowling, the protagonist of this novel, did escaping from a monotonous family life in some pointless London suburb to come back to the places of his childhood. It's just that I don't have an idyllic place to come back to.<br /><br />So, it's mostly about memories, childhood memories, and the bitter but sharp reflections of a fatty man who lost his momentum. But there is also some devastating humour in this book and the foresight of the imminence of World War II.<br /><br />Oh well, the whole list of the reasons why I liked "Coming Up for Air" may take too much time and far too many words to be done. <br />But let's add a last one: I now know the English names of a dozen river fish. I bet you were not expecting this bit of knowledge from a book by Orwell. Weren't you? </span>
  <a data-text-id="12674911660742988813" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_222380608" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/222380608" />
    <a title="Vinny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6623609-vinny"><img alt="Vinny" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/222380608?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vinny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vinny" href="/user/show/6623609-vinny">Vinny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222380608" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1082862695354283071">This book is amazing. I rarely connect on multiply levels with characters in books, but I have a huge connection with Tubby Bowling. I see myself in him, or I should say I could see my life going down his path, because he is disappointed with his life and where is at. He had a lot of big dreams but they all fell one by one to end as an insurance salesman with a decent house in suburbia and a disliked family. This is one of Orwell's forgotten masterpieces in that few look beyond his 1984 or Anima</span>
  <span id="freeText1082862695354283071" style="display:none">This book is amazing. I rarely connect on multiply levels with characters in books, but I have a huge connection with Tubby Bowling. I see myself in him, or I should say I could see my life going down his path, because he is disappointed with his life and where is at. He had a lot of big dreams but they all fell one by one to end as an insurance salesman with a decent house in suburbia and a disliked family. This is one of Orwell's forgotten masterpieces in that few look beyond his 1984 or Animal Farm to see his other works, and that is a shame. The first half of the story is of Tubby reminiscing about his childhood; the second half is his attempt to regain it. The scene where he picks flowers struck me as beautiful. All his actions and thoughts are just about mine. Rarely does somebody bring a character to life for me, but Orwell succeeded with this one. My favorite line was when he was talking about his wife he doesn't like anymore and that is "I actually thought seriously about killing her for the first two years of our marriage, but after that I didn't care enough to do that."<br />	The flowers scene is of Tubby enjoying spring for the first time in a long while, to the point of picking flowers. But as a car of teens drive by, he becomes disillusioned as realizes he is just a fat nobody that will be forgotten by history in a matter of years.<br />	I think this might be a fear of mine, to get stuck in a suburban hell of brat children and an annoying wife. The first part of the book showcases Orwell’s socialist leanings but he lightens up after about 50 pages. This book is a must read for any Orwell fan. Honestly I doubt most people will like this book, but if you enjoy the books I rated well, then you will almost certainly love this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1082862695354283071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2755786082" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2755786082" />
    <a title="Anna Chetwynd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28109654-anna-chetwynd"><img alt="Anna Chetwynd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588152274p2/28109654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2755786082?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Chetwynd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Chetwynd" href="/user/show/28109654-anna-chetwynd">Anna Chetwynd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=london">london</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=britain">britain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=english-class">english-class</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=ww2">ww2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=change-coming">change-coming</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=1930-s">1930-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=upheaval">upheaval</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=life-changing-favorites">life-changing-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28109654-anna-chetwynd?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2755786082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12106371145530657441">How can this book, written on the eve of the Second World War, be so modern in feel? It's extraordinary. The life of an average Everyman George Bowling, who takes a spontaneous trip on the proceeds of some winnings at the races, abandoning wife and the suburbs and heading off on an odyssey to an England that has changed in the space of a few years and is about to change even more brutally very quickly.<br/><br/>Like all Orwell's writing, there is a sense of us watching an apocalypse either happening, abou</span>
  <span id="freeText12106371145530657441" style="display:none">How can this book, written on the eve of the Second World War, be so modern in feel? It's extraordinary. The life of an average Everyman George Bowling, who takes a spontaneous trip on the proceeds of some winnings at the races, abandoning wife and the suburbs and heading off on an odyssey to an England that has changed in the space of a few years and is about to change even more brutally very quickly.<br /><br />Like all Orwell's writing, there is a sense of us watching an apocalypse either happening, about to happen or living in the aftermath of one. With 1984, it was a strange new order, the future imagined, a twisted England, now the base of many dystopian fantasies.<br /><br />But with book is not a dystopia. It is still very much 1930's England, the suburbs a metro land sprawl, the village of his childhood altered but still glimpses of a charming, bucolic delight, full of memories lost but appearing like joyful bubbles amidst the harsh changes he finds elsewhere.<br /><br />And through it all the horrific sense of foreboding, the rise of Fascism apparent in the simple provincial altercations he perceives, mans desire to crush and destroy another played out against a parochial background, the mundane nature of the horror and cruelty to come.<br /><br />An incredible book and totally underrated.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12106371145530657441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17471855889675937803">Well now, this is what I call an interesting book. Orwell is one of my favorite authors. I think pretty much everything he wrote is worth reading but there is so much complexity to this novel. The first thing I thought was the fact that you could see 1984 & Animal Farm coming reading that book. Even thou</span>
  <span id="freeText17471855889675937803" style="display:none">Well now, this is what I call an interesting book. Orwell is one of my favorite authors. I think pretty much everything he wrote is worth reading but there is so much complexity to this novel. The first thing I thought was the fact that you could see 1984 &amp; Animal Farm coming reading that book. The brilliant thing about his writing is that Orwell has such a sharp wit, he is an intellectual and his satire makes everything better. What I didn’t like about Coming Up for Air was the ryhtm. Even though it was both sad and beautiful and also funny at the same time, it doesn’t really get interesting much. It’s all rambling from beginning to the end. To me, this isn’t his best work and I didn’t like it as much as I thought I would but I still think it’s worth reading if you are a fan of Orwell. <br /></span>

<div id="review_2941650195" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2941650195" />
    <a title="Hafizz Nasri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5571752-hafizz-nasri"><img alt="Hafizz Nasri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587304348p2/5571752.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2941650195?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hafizz Nasri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hafizz Nasri" href="/user/show/5571752-hafizz-nasri">Hafizz Nasri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141185699" href="/book/show/832138.Coming_Up_for_Air">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2941650195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2788864368303253623">This started with so much irritating incidents that I was a bit reluctant in furthering my read cause it seems so 'messy' and harsh. It came with four parts, more like stages of how George Bowling starting to have an idea on how to escape his dreary boring life, the point of how it all started. An intro of his family and annoying kids, an insurance salesman job and burden of so much hell he feels like suffocating and while thinking about having his new false teeth and extra quid on his account, </span>
  <span id="freeText2788864368303253623" style="display:none">This started with so much irritating incidents that I was a bit reluctant in furthering my read cause it seems so 'messy' and harsh. It came with four parts, more like stages of how George Bowling starting to have an idea on how to escape his dreary boring life, the point of how it all started. An intro of his family and annoying kids, an insurance salesman job and burden of so much hell he feels like suffocating and while thinking about having his new false teeth and extra quid on his account, George is up for an adventure while 'revisiting' his homeland and childhood years. <br /><br />The part of George reminiscing his early days was my favorite. The army war stuff quite fascinating. I really enjoyed his narration and stories. The fishing hobby thingy was a bit draggy for me but it was somehow fun nevertheless. And back to his current life and plotting his strategy to escape back to Lower Binfield this part was a bit hilarious cause I really feel the excitement and horror of escaping from Hilda from George point of view. <br /><br />I can't really say whether I really love this book or not-- it has flaws, some boring narratives here and there and quite wordy too, but the way Orwell did it was miraculously interesting. The views of society and historical part of England back in 1900s and its change was portrayed well by Orwell. Insightful with no major drama or whatsoever tragedy. Just plain story of a middle-aged man trying to find some joy in life. The last part was my total favorite. Good luck George with your three possibilities (I love how Orwell ended this!). </span>
  <a data-text-id="2788864368303253623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 1502436019" href="/book/show/25578617-coming-up-for-air">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42098789-eve-kay?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2053389859" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7949114475848308995"><b>"The past is a curious thing. It's with you all the time. I suppose an hour never passes without your thinking of things that happened ten or twenty years ago, and yet most of the time it's got no reality --"</b><br/><br/>In very short the story is about a man who leads a very ordinary, quite dull, existence in 1939 England. He looks back on his childhood and takes a trip to where he grew up to reminiscence.<br/><br/>Compared to his other works, yes, it's not his best. But in comparison to other authors, Orwell has the</span>
  <span id="freeText7949114475848308995" style="display:none"><b>"The past is a curious thing. It's with you all the time. I suppose an hour never passes without your thinking of things that happened ten or twenty years ago, and yet most of the time it's got no reality --"</b><br /><br />In very short the story is about a man who leads a very ordinary, quite dull, existence in 1939 England. He looks back on his childhood and takes a trip to where he grew up to reminiscence.<br /><br />Compared to his other works, yes, it's not his best. But in comparison to other authors, Orwell has the best works.<br /><br />Hard to rate since I love Orwell's style, the way he writes and the topics, I enjoyed the story and I love the time in question. I have a huge soft spot for huge soft protagonists who are a little bit dumb. The only stuff I didn't like was this pretty long part quite early on in the story about fishing, the kind of bait you need and so on. It's a shame it was so long and that it was in the early part of the story because it put me off for a while and I struggled to get back in to it. The novel(la) is short so that's a huge minus. <br /><br />What I'm gonna have to do is just go with my heart and compare it to a lot of other nonsense I've read over the years and just simply state that Orwell is one of the best.</span>

<div id="review_463060894" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/463060894" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14431683-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575842374p2/14431683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/463060894?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/14431683-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer463060894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7375615145860987968">The best of Orwell’s pre-war novels. It also seems the most his own. <i>Burmese Days</i> apes Somerset Maugham; <i>A Clergyman’s Daughter</i> apes Joyce; <i>Keep the Aspidistra Flying</i> apes Gissing. But <i>Coming up for Air</i> apes no one. Orwell seems to finally realise the crisp, colloquial tone of his essays and non-fiction is the way forward. There is no feeling of the author getting up on stilts. For the first time - in the novels - the voice is as natural and easy as breathing. It helps that George ‘Fatty’ Bowlin</span>
  <span id="freeText7375615145860987968" style="display:none">The best of Orwell’s pre-war novels. It also seems the most his own. <i>Burmese Days</i> apes Somerset Maugham; <i>A Clergyman’s Daughter</i> apes Joyce; <i>Keep the Aspidistra Flying</i> apes Gissing. But <i>Coming up for Air</i> apes no one. Orwell seems to finally realise the crisp, colloquial tone of his essays and non-fiction is the way forward. There is no feeling of the author getting up on stilts. For the first time - in the novels - the voice is as natural and easy as breathing. It helps that George ‘Fatty’ Bowling is easily the most likeable of Orwell’s human characters - down to earth, modest, self-depreciating, and, unusually, no cynic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7375615145860987968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2296604602" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2296604602" />
    <a title="Ian Mapp" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1598886-ian-mapp"><img alt="Ian Mapp" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291393306p2/1598886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2296604602?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian Mapp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian Mapp" href="/user/show/1598886-ian-mapp">Ian Mapp</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1598886-ian-mapp?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1598886-ian-mapp?shelf=lit-fict">lit-fict</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2296604602" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11339858608026013051">Genuinely cannot believe that an 80 year old book, set on the eve of the WWII, concerning a middle aged man having an existential crisis, is still valid today. But it is in spades.<br/><br/>This book is perfect.<br/><br/>George is 45, married in suburbia with two kids and a wife he dislikes but cannot imagine living without. He's a travelling insurance man - middle class, middle earning, middle of the road. The only thing that marks him out is his weight. He's healthy but tubby.<br/><br/>He wins £17 through an illicit bet hi</span>
  <span id="freeText11339858608026013051" style="display:none">Genuinely cannot believe that an 80 year old book, set on the eve of the WWII, concerning a middle aged man having an existential crisis, is still valid today. But it is in spades.<br /><br />This book is perfect.<br /><br />George is 45, married in suburbia with two kids and a wife he dislikes but cannot imagine living without. He's a travelling insurance man - middle class, middle earning, middle of the road. The only thing that marks him out is his weight. He's healthy but tubby.<br /><br />He wins £17 through an illicit bet his wife knows nothing about and decides to splash it on an impromptu solo holiday back to the village he grew up in and a life of simplicity he misses.<br /><br />After much childhood reminisces and how to got to where he is today (including spells in WW1), he heads back in Lower Binfield - which has changed beyond all recognition and nothing life he expects. After half a week on the lash and half halfheartedly attempting to woo women, buying a fishing rod that never gets user and eventually returning with his tail between his legs.<br /><br />So much to admire. It's funny in a tragi-comedy way. George is quite hilarious and his thought processes constantly raise a laugh. One of literature's great characters.<br /><br />The yearning for a past that cannot be relived is perfect and superbly resonant now. He complains about everything - the beer is not as good as it used to be, the Thames is dirtier, the locals aren't looking after the environment. No-one recognises his name or him on his return. This, written in 1939 could be written today.<br /><br />There's a brooding sense of doom - both in George and Society. WWII is on the horizon and I don't think its a spoiler to say that there is an accidental bombing of the village by the RAF. Three deaths but the inquest determine the results as disappointing. This gives an indication to the humour.<br /><br />There's also foreshadowing to his more famous books (Animal Farm and 1984) with Communists vs. Fascists, kids in gasmasks looking like an army of pigs, and sloganering big government.<br /><br />It's short and needed a good ending - which the book delivers in spades, consistent to the themes throughout.<br /><br />Highly recommended. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11339858608026013051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2618786134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14146318171478150215">I first read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/509080.Coming_Up_for_Air' title='Coming Up for Air by George Orwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Coming Up for Air</a> in about 1966, and it immediately became my favorite high school English book. I enjoyed it much more now. I can't imagine that I understood too much of it when I was fifteen, but I did love the vivid descriptions, the sense of English life before the Great War, and the character of George Bowling. I decided, on his advice, that life probably is a bitch well before I had the experience to know.<br/><br/>My copy, marked five shillings and sixpence, is obviously newer than the</span>
  <span id="freeText14146318171478150215" style="display:none">I first read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/509080.Coming_Up_for_Air" title="Coming Up for Air by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Coming Up for Air</a> in about 1966, and it immediately became my favorite high school English book. I enjoyed it much more now. I can't imagine that I understood too much of it when I was fifteen, but I did love the vivid descriptions, the sense of English life before the Great War, and the character of George Bowling. I decided, on his advice, that life probably is a bitch well before I had the experience to know.<br /><br />My copy, marked five shillings and sixpence, is obviously newer than the one marked three shillings and sixpence in the picture, but thanks to Penguin Books it is still intact.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('m5j5kSR3E9RBlKS/s1cJAQOCXJvr0BtKxICaHn5Th54kLer0STWU/Y1lMogfK14GEkYSSb/9Wb0AyglmRqp9LA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Drawing on numerous unpublished sources, he examines in detail the peculiar friendship between the Oxford mathematician Charles Dodgson (Lewis Carroll) and Alice Liddell, the child for whom he invented the Alice stories, and analyzes how this relationship stirred Carroll s imagination and influenced the creation of Wonderland. It also explains why &quot;Alice in Wonderland&quot; (1865) and its sequel, &quot;Through the Looking-Glass&quot; (1871), took on an unstoppable cultural momentum in the Victorian era and why, a century and a half later, they continue to enthrall and delight readers of all ages.\n\n&quot;The Story of Alice&quot; reveals Carroll as both an innovator and a stodgy traditionalist, entrenched in habits and routines. He had a keen double interest in keeping things moving and keeping them just as they are. (In Looking-Glass Land, Alice must run faster and faster just to stay in one place.) Tracing the development of the Alice books from their inception in 1862 to Liddell s death in 1934, Douglas-Fairhurst also provides a keyhole through which to observe a larger, shifting cultural landscape: the birth of photography, changing definitions of childhood, murky questions about sex and sexuality, and the relationship between Carroll s books and other works of Victorian literature.\n\nIn the stormy transition from the Victorian to the modern era, Douglas-Fairhurst shows, Wonderland became a sheltered world apart, where the line between the actual and the possible was In true emulative and contrarian style, Hitchens is both admiring and aggressive, sympathetic yet critical, taking true measure of his subject as hero and problem. Answering both the detractors and the false claimants, Hitchens tears down the façade of sainthood erected by the hagiographers and rebuts the critics point by point. He examines Orwell and his perspectives on fascism, empire, feminism, and Englishness, as well as his outlook on America, a country and culture towards which he exhibited much ambivalence. \n\nWhether thinking about empires or dictators, race or class, nationalism or popular culture, Orwell\'s moral outlook remains indispensable in a world that has undergone vast changes in the fifty years since his death. Combining the best of Hitchens\'s polemical punch and intellectual elegance in a tightly woven and subtle argument, this book addresses not only why Orwell matters today, but how he will continue to matter in a future, uncertain world. \n\nChristopher Hitchens, one of the most incisive minds of our own age, meets Orwell on the page in this provocative encounter of wit, contention and moral Female readers are key to the future of fic<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10013322162732686619\" style=\"display:none\">Ian McEwan once said, \'When women stop reading, the novel will be dead.\' This book explains how precious fiction is to contemporary women readers, and how they draw on it to tell the stories of their lives. Female readers are key to the future of fiction and--as parents, teachers, and librarians--the glue for a literate society. Women treasure the chance to read alone, but have also gregariously shared reading experiences and memories with mothers, daughters, grandchildren, and female friends. For so many, reading novels and short stories enables them to escape and to spread their wings intellectually and emotionally.\n\nThis book, written by an experienced teacher, scholar of women\'s writing, and literature festival director, draws on over 500 interviews with and questionnaires from women readers and writers. It describes how, where, and when women read fiction, and examines why stories and writers influence the way female readers understand and shape their own life stories. Taylor explores why women are the main buyers and readers of fiction, members of book clubs, attendees at literary festivals, and organisers of days out to fictional sites and writers\' homes. The book analyses the special appeal and changing readership of the genres of romance, erotica, and crime. It also illuminates the reasons for women\'s abiding love of two favourite novels, Pride and Prejudice and Jane Eyre. Taylor offers a cornucopia of witty and wise women\'s voices, of both readers themselves and also writers such as Hilary Mantel, Helen Dunmore, Katie Fforde, and Sarah Dunant. The book helps us understand why--in Jackie Kay\'s words--\'our lives are mapped by books.\'<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10013322162732686619\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return The collection contains Stevenson\'s first published fiction, and a few of the stories are considered by some critics to be his best work, as well as pioneering works in the English short story tradition.\n\nThe title is an allusion to the collection of tales known as the One Thousand and One Nights, which Stevenson had read and liked. Although Stevenson\'s stories were set in modern Europe, he was stylistically drawing a connection to the nested structure of the Arabian tales.\n\nNew Arabian Nights is divided into two volumes.\n\nVolume 1 - The first volume contains seven stories originally called Later-day Arabian Nights and published by London Magazine in serial format from June to October 1878. The beautiful age of glamour and pleasure more often showed its ugly side: hysterical, narcissistic, decadent and violent, a time of rampant prejudice and blood-and-soil nativism, with more parallels to our own age than we might imagine.\n\nThe Man in the Red Coat is at once a fresh and original portrait of the Belle Epoque – its heroes and villains, its writers, artists and thinkers – and a life of a man ahead of his time. <span id="freeTextContainer16679410126515229949"><b>Eric Arthur Blair</b>, better known by his pen name <b>George Orwell</b>, was an English author and journalist. His work is marked by keen intelligence and wit, a profound awareness of social injustice, an intense opposition to totalitarianism, a passion for clarity in language, and a belief in democratic socialism.<br/><br/>In addition to his literary career Orwell served as a police officer with the Indian Imperial </span>
  <span id="freeText16679410126515229949" style="display:none"><b>Eric Arthur Blair</b>, better known by his pen name <b>George Orwell</b>, was an English author and journalist. His work is marked by keen intelligence and wit, a profound awareness of social injustice, an intense opposition to totalitarianism, a passion for clarity in language, and a belief in democratic socialism.<br /><br />In addition to his literary career Orwell served as a police officer with the Indian Imperial Police in Burma from 1922-1927 and fought with the Republicans in the Spanish Civil War from 1936-1937. Orwell was severely wounded when he was shot through his throat. Later the organization that he had joined when he joined the Republican cause, The Workers Party of Marxist Unification (POUM), was painted by the pro-Soviet Communists as a Trotskyist organization (Trotsky was Joseph Stalin's enemy) and disbanded. Orwell and his wife were accused of "rabid Trotskyism" and tried in absentia in Barcelona, along with other leaders of the POUM, in 1938. However by then they had escaped from Spain and returned to England. <br /><br />Between 1941 and 1943, Orwell worked on propaganda for the BBC. In 1943, he became literary editor of the Tribune, a weekly left-wing magazine. He was a prolific polemical journalist, article writer, literary critic, reviewer, poet, and writer of fiction, and, considered perhaps the twentieth century's best chronicler of English culture. <br /><br />Orwell is best known for the dystopian novel <i>Nineteen Eighty-Four</i> (published in 1949) and the satirical novella <i>Animal Farm</i> (1945) — they have together sold more copies than any two books by any other twentieth-century author. His 1938 book <i>Homage to Catalonia</i>, an account of his experiences as a volunteer on the Republican side during the Spanish Civil War, together with numerous essays on politics, literature, language, and culture, have been widely acclaimed.<br /> <br />Orwell's influence on contemporary culture, popular and political, continues decades after his death. Several of his neologisms, along with the term "Orwellian" — now a byword for any oppressive or manipulative social phenomenon opposed to a free society — have entered the vernacular.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16679410126515229949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
